A distribution p𝑝p over a discrete alphabet 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X} of size k𝑘k corresponds toan element of the simplexΔ𝒳:={p∈ℝ≥0k:∑x∈𝒳p​(x)=1}.assignsubscriptΔ𝒳conditional-set𝑝superscriptsubscriptℝabsent0𝑘subscript𝑥𝒳𝑝𝑥1\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}:=\left\{p\in\mathbb{R}_{\geq 0}^{k}:\sum_{x\in\mathcal{X}}p(x)=1\right\}.A distribution property is amapping f:Δ𝒳→ℝ:𝑓→subscriptΔ𝒳ℝf:\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}\to\mathbb{R}associating a real value with each distribution.For example its support size.A distribution property f𝑓f is symmetric if it is invariant underdomain-symbol permutations. A symmetric property is additiveif it can be written as f​(p):=∑xf​(p​(x))assign𝑓𝑝subscript𝑥𝑓𝑝𝑥f(p):=\sum_{x}f(p(x)),where for simplicity we use f𝑓f to denote both the property and thecorresponding real function.

Many important symmetric propertiesare additive. For example,•Support size S​(p):=∑x𝟙p​(x)>0assign𝑆𝑝subscript𝑥subscript1𝑝𝑥0S(p):=\sum_{x}\mathbbm{1}_{p(x)>0}, afundamental quantity arising in the study of vocabulary size [31, 57, 74], population estimation [37, 55], and database studies [40].•Support coverage Cm​(p):=∑x(1−(1−p​(x))m)assignsubscript𝐶𝑚𝑝subscript𝑥1superscript1𝑝𝑥𝑚C_{m}(p):=\sum_{x}(1-(1-p(x))^{m}), where m𝑚m is a given parameter, theexpected number of distinct elements observed in a sample of size m𝑚m, arising inbiological [18, 52] and ecological [18, 19, 20, 25] research;•Shannon entropy H​(p):=−∑xp​(x)​log⁡p​(x)assign𝐻𝑝subscript𝑥𝑝𝑥𝑝𝑥H(p):=-\sum_{x}p(x)\log p(x), theprimary measure of information [26, 72] with numerousapplications to machine learning [14, 24, 69] andneuroscience [32, 54];•Distance to uniformity D​(p):=∥p−pu∥1assign𝐷𝑝subscriptdelimited-∥∥𝑝subscript𝑝𝑢1D(p):=\left\lVert p-p_{u}\right\rVert_{1}, where pusubscript𝑝𝑢p_{u} is the uniform distribution over 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X},a property being central to the field of distributionproperty testing [11, 12, 15, 71].

Besides being symmetric and additive, these four properties haveyet another attribute in common. Under the appropriate interpretation,they are also all 111-Lipschitz.Specifically, for two distributions p,q∈Δ𝒳𝑝𝑞subscriptΔ𝒳p,q\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}},let Γp,qsubscriptΓ𝑝𝑞\Gamma_{p,q} be the collection of distributions over𝒳×𝒳𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{X} with marginals p𝑝p and q𝑞q on the first and second factors respectively.The relative earth-mover distance [76], between p𝑝p andq𝑞q isR​(p,q):=infγ∈Γp,q𝔼(X,Y)∼γ|log⁡p​(X)q​(Y)|.assign𝑅𝑝𝑞subscriptinfimum𝛾subscriptΓ𝑝𝑞subscript𝔼similar-to𝑋𝑌𝛾𝑝𝑋𝑞𝑌R(p,q):=\inf_{\gamma\in\Gamma_{p,q}}\;\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{(X,Y)\sim\gamma}\left|\log\frac{p(X)}{q(Y)}\right|.One can verify [76, 77] thatH𝐻H, D𝐷D, and C~m:=Cm/massignsubscript~𝐶𝑚subscript𝐶𝑚𝑚\tilde{C}_{m}:=C_{m}/m are all 111-Lipschitzon the metricspace (Δ𝒳,R)subscriptΔ𝒳𝑅(\Delta_{\mathcal{X}},R), andS~:=S/kassign~𝑆𝑆𝑘\tilde{S}:=S/k is 111-Lipschitz over (Δ≥1/k,R)subscriptΔabsent1𝑘𝑅(\Delta_{\geq{1}/{k}},R),the set of distributions inΔ𝒳subscriptΔ𝒳\Delta_{\mathcal{X}} whose nonzero probabilities are at least 1/k1𝑘1/{k}.We will study all such Lipschitz properties in later sections.

An important symmetric non-additive property is Rényi entropy, a well-known measure ofrandomness with numerous applications to unsupervised learning [46, 86] and image registration [53, 58]. For a distributionp∈Δ𝒳𝑝subscriptΔ𝒳p\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}} and a non-negative real parameter α≠1𝛼1\alpha\not=1, the α𝛼\alpha-Rényi entropy [70] of p𝑝p is Hα​(p):=(1−α)−1​log⁡(∑xp​(x)α)assignsubscript𝐻𝛼𝑝superscript1𝛼1subscript𝑥𝑝superscript𝑥𝛼H_{\alpha}(p):=(1-\alpha)^{-1}\log\left(\sum_{x}p(x)^{\alpha}\right).In particular, denoted by H1​(p):=limα→1Hα​(p)assignsubscript𝐻1𝑝subscript→𝛼1subscript𝐻𝛼𝑝H_{1}(p):=\lim_{\alpha\to 1}H_{\alpha}(p), the 111-Rényi entropy is exactly Shannon entropy [70].

We consider the following three fundamental statistical-learning problems.

A natural learning problem is to estimate an unknown distribution p∈Δ𝒳𝑝subscriptΔ𝒳p\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}} from an i.i.d. sample Xn∼psimilar-tosuperscript𝑋𝑛𝑝X^{n}\sim p. A closely related problem is distribution property testing, of which identity testing is the most fundamental and well-studied [15, 34]. Originating from the principle of maximum likelihood,PML was proved [2, 5, 7, 27, 62]to possessa number ofuseful attributes, such asexistence over finite discrete domains,majorization by empirical distributions,consistency for distribution estimation under both sorted and unsorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distances,and competitiveness to other profile-based estimators.

[21] constructed an explicit exp⁡(−𝒪​(n2/3​log3⁡n))𝒪superscript𝑛23superscript3𝑛\exp(-\mathcal{O}({n}^{2/3}\log^{3}n))-approximate PML (APML) whose computation time is near-linear in n𝑛n. Combined, these two results provide a unified, sample-optimal, and near-linear-time computable plug-in estimator for the four properties.

The recent works of Diakonikolas and Kane [28] and Goldreich [33] provided a procedure reducing identity testing to uniformity testing, while modifying the desired accuracy and alphabet size by only absolute constant factors. Hence below we consider uniformity testing.

The rest of the paper is organized as follows. Section 3 and Section 4 illustrate PML’s theoretical and practical advantages by comparing it to existingmethods for a variety of learning tasks. With the exception of Section 8, which proposes TPML and establishes four new results, Section 5 to 9 present the proofs of Theorem 1 to 6. Section 10 concludes the paper and outlines future directions.

Several immediate implications are in order.

Theorem 1 makes PML the first plug-in estimator that is universally sample-optimal for a broad class of distribution properties.In particular, Theorem 1 also covers the four properties considered in [5]. To see this, as mentioned in Section 1.1, C~msubscript~𝐶𝑚\tilde{C}_{m}, H𝐻H, and D𝐷D are 111-Lipschitz on (Δ𝒳,R)subscriptΔ𝒳𝑅(\Delta_{\mathcal{X}},R); as for S~~𝑆\tilde{S}, the following result [5] relates it to C~msubscript~𝐶𝑚\tilde{C}_{m} for distributions in Δ≥1/ksubscriptΔabsent1𝑘\Delta_{\geq{1}/{k}}, and proves PML’s optimality.

The theorem also applies to many other properties. As an example [76], given an integer s>0𝑠0s>0, let fs​(x):=min⁡{x,|x−1/s|}assignsubscript𝑓𝑠𝑥𝑥𝑥1𝑠f_{s}(x):=\min\{x,|x-1/s|\}. Then to within a factor of two, fs​(p):=∑xfs​(p​(x))assignsubscript𝑓𝑠𝑝subscript𝑥subscript𝑓𝑠𝑝𝑥f_{s}(p):=\sum_{x}f_{s}(p(x)) approximates the ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance between any distribution p𝑝p and the closest uniform distribution in Δ𝒳subscriptΔ𝒳\Delta_{\mathcal{X}} of support size s𝑠s.

In Section 3.2 we compare Theorem 1 with existing results and present more of its implications.

Theorem 2 and 3 imply that for allnon-integer α>3/4𝛼34\alpha>3/4 (resp. α>5/6𝛼56\alpha>5/6), the PML (resp. APML) plug-in estimator achieves a sample complexity better than the best currently known [6]. This makes both the PML and APML plug-in estimators the state-of-the-art algorithms for estimating non-integer order Rényi entropy.See Section 3.3 for an introduction of known results, and see Section 3.4 for a detailed comparison between existing methods and ours.

Theorem 4 shows that for all integer α>1𝛼1\alpha>1, the sample complexity of the PML plug-in estimator has optimal k1−1/αsuperscript𝑘11𝛼k^{1-1/\alpha} dependence [6, 59] on the alphabet size k𝑘k.

Theorem 5 makes APML the first distributionestimator under sorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance that is both near-linear-timecomputable and sample-optimal for a range of desired accuracyε𝜀\varepsilon beyond inverse polylogarithmic of n𝑛n. In comparison, existingalgorithms [2, 41, 79] either run in polynomial time inthe sample sizes, or are only known to achieve optimal sample complexity for ε=Ω​(1/log⁡n)𝜀Ω1𝑛\varepsilon=\Omega(1/\sqrt{\log n}), which is essentially different from the applicable range of ε≥n−Θ​(1)𝜀superscript𝑛Θ1\varepsilon\geq n^{-\Theta(1)} in Theorem 5. We provide a more detailed comparison in Section 3.6.

Theorem 6provides the first PML-based uniformity tester with near-optimal sample complexity. As stated, the tester also distinguishes between p=pu𝑝subscript𝑝𝑢p=p_{u} and ∥p−pu∥2≥ε/ksubscriptdelimited-∥∥𝑝subscript𝑝𝑢2𝜀𝑘\left\lVert p-p_{u}\right\rVert_{2}\geq\varepsilon/\sqrt{k}.This is a stronger guarantee since by the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality,∥p−pu∥1≥εsubscriptdelimited-∥∥𝑝subscript𝑝𝑢1𝜀\left\lVert p-p_{u}\right\rVert_{1}\geq\varepsilon implies ∥p−pu∥2≥ε/ksubscriptdelimited-∥∥𝑝subscript𝑝𝑢2𝜀𝑘\left\lVert p-p_{u}\right\rVert_{2}\geq\varepsilon/\sqrt{k}.

The study of additive property estimation dates back at least half acentury [16, 37, 38] and has steadily grown over theyears. For any additive symmetric property f𝑓f and sequence xnsuperscript𝑥𝑛x^{n}, thesimplest and most widely-used approach uses the empirical(plug-in) estimator f^E​(xn):=f​(pμ​(xn))assignsuperscript^𝑓𝐸superscript𝑥𝑛𝑓subscript𝑝𝜇superscript𝑥𝑛\hat{f}^{E}(x^{n}):=f(p_{\mu}(x^{n}))that evaluates f𝑓f at the empirical distribution.While the empirical estimatorperforms well in the large-sample regime, modern data science applications oftenconcern high-dimensional data, for which more involved methods have yielded propertyestimators that are more sample-efficient.For example, for relatively large k𝑘k and for f𝑓f being S~~𝑆\tilde{S}, C~msubscript~𝐶𝑚\tilde{C}_{m},H𝐻H, or D𝐷D, recent research [47, 64, 75, 76, 84, 85] showed thatthe empirical estimator is optimal up to logarithmic factors, namelynf​(𝒫,ε)=Θε​(nf​(f^E,𝒫,ε)/log⁡nf​(f^E,𝒫,ε)),subscript𝑛𝑓𝒫𝜀subscriptΘ𝜀subscript𝑛𝑓superscript^𝑓𝐸𝒫𝜀subscript𝑛𝑓superscript^𝑓𝐸𝒫𝜀n_{f}(\mathcal{P},\varepsilon)=\Theta_{\varepsilon}({n_{f}(\hat{f}^{E},\mathcal{P},\varepsilon)}/{\log n_{f}(\hat{f}^{E},\mathcal{P},\varepsilon)}),where 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} is Δ≥1/ksubscriptΔabsent1𝑘\Delta_{\geq{1}/{k}} for S~~𝑆\tilde{S},and is Δ𝒳subscriptΔ𝒳\Delta_{\mathcal{X}} for the other properties.

Below we classifythe methods for deriving the corresponding sample-optimal estimators into twocategories: plug-in and approximation, and provide a high-leveldescription. For simplicity of illustration, we assumethat ε∈(0,1]𝜀01\varepsilon\in(0,1].

The plug-in approach essentially estimates the unknown distribution multiset,which suffices for computing anysymmetric properties. Besides the empirical and PMLestimators,Efron and Thisted [31] proposed a linear-programming approach that finds a multiset estimate consistent with the sample’s profile. This approach was then adapted and analyzed by Valiant and Valiant [75, 79], yielding plug-in estimators that achieve near-optimal sample complexities for H𝐻H and S~~𝑆\tilde{S}, and optimal sample complexity for D𝐷D, when ε𝜀\varepsilon is relatively large.

The approximation approach modifies non-smooth segments of the probability function to correct the bias of empirical estimators. A popular modification is to replace those non-smooth segments by their low-degree polynomial approximations and then estimate the modified function.For several properties including the above four and power sum Pα​(p):=∑xp​(x)αassignsubscript𝑃𝛼𝑝subscript𝑥𝑝superscript𝑥𝛼P_{\alpha}(p):=\sum_{x}p(x)^{\alpha}, where α𝛼\alpha is a given parameter,this approach yields property-dependent estimators [47, 64, 84, 85] that are sample-optimal for allε𝜀\varepsilon.

More recently, Acharya et al. [5] proved the aforementioned results on PML estimator and made it the first unified, sample-optimal plug-in estimator for S~~𝑆\tilde{S}, C~msubscript~𝐶𝑚\tilde{C}_{m}, H𝐻H and D𝐷D and relatively large ε𝜀\varepsilon.Following these advances, Han et al. [41] refined the linear-programming approach and designed a plug-in estimator that implicitly performs polynomial approximation and is sample-optimal for H𝐻H, S~~𝑆\tilde{S}, and Pαsubscript𝑃𝛼P_{\alpha} with α<1𝛼1\alpha<1, when ε𝜀\varepsilon is relatively large.

In terms of the estimator’s theoretical guarantee,Theorem 1 is essentially the sameas Valiant and Valiant [76]. However,for each property, k𝑘k, and n𝑛n, [76] solves a different linear program and constructs a new estimator, which takes polynomial time.On the other hand, both the PML estimator and its near-linear-time computable variant, once computed, can be used to accurately estimate exponentially many properties that are 111-Lipschitz on (Δ𝒳,R)subscriptΔ𝒳𝑅(\Delta_{\mathcal{X}},R). A similar comparison holds between the PML method and the approximation approach, while the latter is provably sample-optimal for only a few properties. In addition, Theorem 1 shows that the PML estimator often achieves the optimal sample complexity up to a small constant factor, which is a desired estimator attribute shared by some, but not all approximation-based estimators [47, 64, 84, 85].

In term of the method and proof technique, Theorem 1 isclosest to Acharya et al. [5]. On the other hand, [5]establishes the optimality of PML for onlyfour properties, while our result covers a much broader property class.In addition, both the above mentioned “small constant factor” attribute, and the confidence boost from 2/3232/3 to 1−exp⁡(−4​n)14𝑛1-\exp(-4\sqrt{n}) are unique contributions of this work.The PML plug-in approach is also close in flavor to the plug-in estimators in Valiant and Valiant [75, 79] and their refinement in Han et al. [41].On the other hand, as pointed out previously, these plug-in estimators are provably sample-optimal for only a few properties.More specifically, for estimating H𝐻H, S~~𝑆\tilde{S}, and C~msubscript~𝐶𝑚\tilde{C}_{m},the plug-in estimators in [75, 79] achieve sub-optimalsample complexities with regard to the desired accuracy ε𝜀\varepsilon;and the estimation guarantee in [41] is provided in terms ofthe approximation errors of 𝒪~​(n)~𝒪𝑛\tilde{\mathcal{O}}(\sqrt{n})polynomials that are not directly related to the optimal samplecomplexities.

Motivated by the wide applications of Rényi entropy, heuristic estimators were proposed and studiedin the physics literature following [39], and asymptotically consistent estimators were presented andanalyzed in the statistical-learning literature [48, 87].For the special case of 1-Rényi (or Shannon) entropy, the works of [75, 76] determined the sample complexity to be nf​(ε)=Θ​(k/(ε​log⁡k))subscript𝑛𝑓𝜀Θ𝑘𝜀𝑘n_{f}(\varepsilon)=\Theta(k/(\varepsilon\log k)).

For general α𝛼\alpha-Rényi entropy, the best-known results in Acharya et al. [6] state thatfor integer and non-integer α𝛼\alpha values, the corresponding sample complexities nf​(ε,δ)subscript𝑛𝑓𝜀𝛿n_{f}(\varepsilon,\delta) are 𝒪α​(k1−1/α​log⁡(1/δ)/ε2)subscript𝒪𝛼superscript𝑘11𝛼1𝛿superscript𝜀2\mathcal{O}_{\alpha}(k^{1-1/\alpha}\log(1/\delta)/\varepsilon^{2}) and 𝒪α​(kmin⁡{1/α,1}​log⁡(1/δ)/(ε1/α​log⁡k))subscript𝒪𝛼superscript𝑘1𝛼11𝛿superscript𝜀1𝛼𝑘\mathcal{O}_{\alpha}(k^{\min\{1/\alpha,1\}}\log(1/\delta)/(\varepsilon^{1/\alpha}\log k)), respectively.The upper bounds for integer α𝛼\alpha are achieved by an estimator that corrects the bias of the empirical plug-in estimator.To achieve the upper bounds for non-integer α𝛼\alpha values, one needs to compute some best polynomial approximation of zαsuperscript𝑧𝛼z^{\alpha}, whose degree and domain both depend on n𝑛n, andconstruct a more involved estimator using the approximation approach [47, 84] mentioned in Section 3.1.

Our result shows that a single PML estimate suffices to estimate the Rényi entropy of different orders α𝛼\alpha. Such adaptiveness to the order parameter is a significant advantage of PML over existing methods.For example, by Theorem 3 and the union bound, one can use a single APML or PML to accurately approximate exponentially many non-integer order Rényi entropy values, yet still maintains an overall confidence of 1−exp⁡(−k0.9)1superscript𝑘0.91-\exp(-k^{0.9}).By comparison, the estimation heuristic in [6] requires different polynomial-based estimators for different α𝛼\alpha values. In particular, to construct each estimator, one needs to compute some best polynomial approximation of zαsuperscript𝑧𝛼z^{\alpha}, which is not known to admit a closed-form formula for α∉ℤ𝛼ℤ\alpha\not\in\mathbb{Z}. Furthermore, even for a single α𝛼\alpha and with a sample size k𝑘\sqrt{k} times larger, such estimator is not known to achieve the same level of confidence as PML or APML.

As for the theoretical guarantees, the sample-complexity upper bounds in both Theorem 2 and 3 are better than those mentioned in the previous section. More specifically,for any α∈(3/4,1)𝛼341\alpha\in(3/4,1) and δ≥exp⁡(−k0.5)𝛿superscript𝑘0.5\delta\geq\exp(-k^{0.5}), Theorem 2 shows that nf​(ε,δ)=𝒪α​(k1/α/(ε1/α​log⁡k))subscript𝑛𝑓𝜀𝛿subscript𝒪𝛼superscript𝑘1𝛼superscript𝜀1𝛼𝑘n_{f}(\varepsilon,\delta)=\mathcal{O}_{\alpha}(k^{1/\alpha}/(\varepsilon^{1/\alpha}\log k)). Analogously, for any non-integer α>1𝛼1\alpha>1 and δ≥exp⁡(−k0.9)𝛿superscript𝑘0.9\delta\geq\exp(-k^{0.9}), Theorem 3 shows that nf​(ε,δ)=𝒪α​(k/(ε1/α​log⁡k))subscript𝑛𝑓𝜀𝛿subscript𝒪𝛼𝑘superscript𝜀1𝛼𝑘n_{f}(\varepsilon,\delta)=\mathcal{O}_{\alpha}(k/(\varepsilon^{1/\alpha}\log k)). Both bounds are better than the best currently known by a log⁡(1/δ)1𝛿\log(1/\delta) factor.

Estimating large-alphabet distributions from their samplesis a fundamental statistical-learning tenet.Over the past few decades, distribution estimation has found numerousapplications, ranging from natural language modeling [22] to biological research [8], and has been studied extensively.Under the classical ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} and KL losses, existing research [13, 49] showed that the corresponding sample complexities n​(ε)𝑛𝜀n(\varepsilon) are Θ​(k/ε2)Θ𝑘superscript𝜀2\Theta(k/\varepsilon^{2}) and Θ​(k/ε)Θ𝑘𝜀\Theta(k/\varepsilon), respectively.Several recent works have investigated the analogous formulation under sorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance, and revealed a lower sample complexity of n​(ε)=Θ​(k/(ε2​log⁡k))𝑛𝜀Θ𝑘superscript𝜀2𝑘n(\varepsilon)=\Theta(k/(\varepsilon^{2}\log k)).Specifically, under certain conditions, Valiant and Valiant [79] and Han et al. [41] derived sample-optimal estimators using linear programming, and Acharya et al. [2] showed that PML achieves a sub-optimal 𝒪​(k/(ε2.1​log⁡k))𝒪𝑘superscript𝜀2.1𝑘\mathcal{O}(k/(\varepsilon^{2.1}\log k)) sample complexity for relatively large ε𝜀\varepsilon.

We compare our results with existing ones from three differentperspectives.

Applicable parameter ranges: As shown by [41], for ε≪n−1/3much-less-than𝜀superscript𝑛13\varepsilon\ll n^{-1/3}, thesimple empirical estimator is already sample-optimal. Hence weconsider the parammeter range ε=Ω​(n−1/3)𝜀Ωsuperscript𝑛13\varepsilon=\Omega(n^{-1/3}).For the results in [79] and [2] to hold, we would need ε𝜀\varepsilon to be at least Ω​(1/log⁡n)Ω1𝑛\Omega(1/\sqrt{\log n}).On the other hand, Theorem 5 shows that PML and APML are sample-optimal for ε𝜀\varepsilon larger than n−Θ​(1)superscript𝑛Θ1n^{-\Theta(1)}. Here, the gap is exponentially large. The result in [41] applies to the whole range ε=Ω​(n−1/3)𝜀Ωsuperscript𝑛13\varepsilon=\Omega(n^{-1/3}), which is larger than the applicable range of our results.

Time complexity: Both the APML and the estimator in [79] are near-linear-time computable in the sample sizes, while the estimator in [41] would require polynomial time to be computed.

Statistical confidence: The PML and APML achieve the desiredaccuracy with an error probability at most exp⁡(−Ω​(n))Ω𝑛\exp(-\Omega(\sqrt{n})). On the contrary, the estimatorin [41] is known to achieve an error probability thatdecreases only as 𝒪​(n−3)𝒪superscript𝑛3\mathcal{O}(n^{-3}). The gap is again exponentially large.The estimator in [79] admits an error probability bound of exp⁡(−n0.02)superscript𝑛0.02\exp(-n^{0.02}), which is still far from ours.

Initiated by the work of [35], identity testing is arguably one of the most important and widely-studied problems in distribution property testing. Over the past two decades, a sequence of works [3, 4, 12, 17, 28, 29, 30, 35, 67, 78] have addressed the sample complexity of this problem and proposed testers with a variety of guarantees. In particular, applying a coincidence-based tester, Paninski [67] determined the sample complexity of uniformity testing up to constant factors; utilizing a variant of the Pearson’s chi-squared statistic, Valiant and Valiant [78] resolved the general identity testing problem.For an overview of related results, we refer interested readersto [15] and [34].The contribution of this work is mainly showing that PML, is a unifiedsample-optimal approach for several related problems, and as shown inTheorem 6, also provides a near-optimal tester for this important testing problem.

A number of different approaches have been taken to computing the PML and its approximations.Among the existing works, Acharya et al. [1] considered exact algebraic computation, Orlitsky et al. [61, 62] designed an EM algorithm with MCMC acceleration, Vontobel [82, 83] proposed a Bethe approximation heuristic, Anevski et al. [7] introduced a sieved PML estimator and a stochastic approximation of the associated EM algorithm, and Pavlichin et al. [68] derived a dynamic programming approach. Notably and recently, for a sample size n𝑛n, Charikar et al. [21] constructed an explicit exp⁡(−𝒪​(n2/3​log3⁡n))𝒪superscript𝑛23superscript3𝑛\exp(-\mathcal{O}({n}^{2/3}\log^{3}n))-approximate PML whose computation time is near-linear in n𝑛n.

In this section, we first introduce a variant of the MCMC-EM algorithm in [61, 62, 65] and then demonstrate theefficacy of PML on a variety of learning tasks through experiments.

To approximate PML, the work [61] proposed an MCMC-EM algorithm, where MCMC and EM stand for Markov chain Monte Carlo and expectation maximization, respectively. A sketch of the original MCMC-EM algorithm can be found in [61], and a detailed description is available in Chapter 6 of [65]. The EM part uses a simple iteration procedure to update the distribution estimates. One can show [65] that it is equivalent to the conventional generalized gradient ascent method.The MCMC part exploits local properties of the update process and accelerates the EM computation. Below we present a variant of this algorithm that often runs faster and is more accurate.

We separate the large and small multiplicities. Define a threshold parameter τ:=1.5​log2⁡nassign𝜏1.5superscript2𝑛\tau:=1.5\log^{2}n and suppress Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n} in pμ​(Xn)subscript𝑝𝜇superscript𝑋𝑛p_{\mu}(X^{n}) for simplicity. For symbols x𝑥x with μx​(Xn)≥τsubscript𝜇𝑥superscript𝑋𝑛𝜏\mu_{x}(X^{n})\geq\tau, estimate their probabilities by pμ​(x)=μx​(Xn)/nsubscript𝑝𝜇𝑥subscript𝜇𝑥superscript𝑋𝑛𝑛p_{\mu}(x)=\mu_{x}(X^{n})/n and remove them from the sample. Denote the collection of removed symbols by R𝑅R and the remaining sample sequence by Xrsuperscript𝑋𝑟X^{r}. In the subsequent steps, we apply the EM-MCMC algorithm to Xrsuperscript𝑋𝑟X^{r}.

The idea is simple: By the Chernoff-type bound for binomial random variables, with high probability, theempirical frequency μx​(Xn)/nsubscript𝜇𝑥superscript𝑋𝑛𝑛\mu_{x}(X^{n})/n of a large-multiplicity symbol x𝑥xis very close to its mean value p​(x)𝑝𝑥p(x).Hence for large-multiplicity symbols we can simply use the empiricalestimates and focus on estimating the probabilities ofsmall-multiplicitysymbols. This is similar to initializing the EMalgorithm by the empirical distribution and fixing the largeprobability estimates through the iterations. However, the approachdescribed here is more efficient.

We determine a proper alphabet size for the outputdistribution of the EM algorithm. If the true value k𝑘k is provided,then we simply use k−|R|𝑘𝑅k-|R|. Otherwise, we apply the following supportsize estimator [5] to Xrsuperscript𝑋𝑟X^{r}:S^​(Xr):=∑j≥1(1−(−(t−1))j​Pr⁡(L≥j))⋅φj​(Xr),assign^𝑆superscript𝑋𝑟subscript𝑗1⋅1superscript𝑡1𝑗Pr𝐿𝑗subscript𝜑𝑗superscript𝑋𝑟\hat{S}(X^{r}):=\sum_{j\geq 1}(1-(-(t-1))^{j}\Pr(L\geq j))\cdot\varphi_{j}(X^{r}),where t=log⁡r𝑡𝑟t=\log r and L𝐿L is an independent binomial random variable with support size ⌈12​log2⁡(r​t2t−1)⌉12subscript2𝑟superscript𝑡2𝑡1\lceil\frac{1}{2}\log_{2}(\frac{rt^{2}}{t-1})\rceil and success probability (t+1)−1superscript𝑡11{(t+1)^{-1}}. For any ε𝜀\varepsilon larger than an absolute constant, estimator S^^𝑆\hat{S} achieves the optimal sample complexity nf​(Δ≥1/k,ε)subscript𝑛𝑓subscriptΔabsent1𝑘𝜀n_{f}(\Delta_{\geq 1/k},\varepsilon) in estimating support size, up to constant factors [5].

Apply the MCMC-EM algorithm in [61, 65] to φ​(Xr)𝜑superscript𝑋𝑟\varphi(X^{r}) with the output alphabet size determined in the previous step, and denote the resulting distribution estimate by prsubscript𝑝𝑟p_{r}. (In the experiments, we perform the EM iteration for 303030 times.)Intuitively, this estimate corresponds to the conditional distribution given that the next observation is a symbol with small probability.

Let Tμ:=∑x∈Rpμ​(x)assignsubscript𝑇𝜇subscript𝑥𝑅subscript𝑝𝜇𝑥T_{\mu}:=\sum_{x\in R}p_{\mu}(x) be the totalprobability of the large-multiplicity symbols. Treat prsubscript𝑝𝑟p_{r} as a vectorand let pr′:=(1−Tr)⋅prassignsuperscriptsubscript𝑝𝑟′⋅1subscript𝑇𝑟subscript𝑝𝑟p_{r}^{\prime}:=(1-T_{r})\cdot p_{r}. For every symbol x∈R𝑥𝑅x\in R, appendpμ​(x)subscript𝑝𝜇𝑥p_{\mu}(x) to pr′superscriptsubscript𝑝𝑟′p_{r}^{\prime}, and return the resulting vector. Note that thisvector corresponds to a valid discrete distribution.

The implementation of our algorithm is available at https://github.com/ucsdyi/PML.

For computational efficiency, the program code for the original MCMC-EM algorithm in [61, 65] is written in C++, with a file name “MCMCEM.cpp”. The program code for other functions is written in Python3. Note that to execute the program, one should have a 64-bit Windows/Linux system with Python3 installed (64-bit version). In addition, we also use functions provided by “NumPy” and “SciPy”, while the latter is not crucial and can be removed by modifying the code slightly.

Our implementation also makes use of “ctypes”, a built-inforeign language library for Python that allows us to call C++ functions directly. Note that before calling C++ functions in Python, we need to compile the corresponding C++ source files into DLLs or shared libraries.We have compiled and included two such files, one is “MCMCEM.so”, the other is “MCMCEM.dll”.

Functions in “MCMCEM.cpp” can be used separately. To compute a PML estimate, simply call the function “int PML(int MAXSZ=10000, int maximum_EM=20, int EM_n=100)”, where the first parameter specifies an upper bound on the support size of theoutput distribution, the second provides the maximum number of EM iteration, and the last corresponds to the sample size n𝑛n.This function takes as input a local file called “proFile”, which contains the profile vector φ​(Xn)𝜑superscript𝑋𝑛\varphi(X^{n}) in the format of “1 4 7 10 …”. Specifically, the file “proFile” consists of only space-separated non-negative integers, and the i𝑖i-th integer represents the value of φi​(Xn)subscript𝜑𝑖superscript𝑋𝑛\varphi_{i}(X^{n}). The output is a vector of length at most MAXSZ, and is stored in another local file called “PMLFile”. Each line of the file “PMLFile” contains a non-negative real number, corresponding to a probability estimate.

To perform experiments and save the plots to the directory containingthe code, simply execute the file “Main.py”. To avoid furthercomplication, the code compares our estimator with only three otherestimators: empirical, empirical with a larger n​log⁡n𝑛𝑛n\log n sample size, and improved Good-Turing [60] (for distribution estimation under unsorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance). The implementation covers all the distributions described in the next section. One can test any of these distributions by including it in “D_List” of the “main()” function.The implementation also covers a variety of learning tasks, such as distribution estimation under sorted and unsorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distances, and property estimation for Shannon entropy, α𝛼\alpha-Rényi entropy, support coverage, and support size.

Finally, functions related to distribution and sample generation are available in file “Samples.py”. Others including the property computation functions, the sorted and unsorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance functions, and the previously-described support size estimator, are contained in file “Functions.py”.

In the following experiments, samples are generated according to six distributions with the same support size k=5,000𝑘5000k=5{,}000.

Three of them have finite support by definition:uniform distribution,two-step distribution with half the symbols having probability 2/(5​k)25𝑘2/(5k) and the other half have probability 8/(5​k)85𝑘8/(5k),and a three-step distribution with one third the symbols havingprobability 3/(13​k)313𝑘3/(13k), another third having probability 9/(13​k)913𝑘9/(13k),and the remaining having probability 27/(13​k)2713𝑘27/(13k).

The other three distributions are over {i∈ℤ:i≥1}conditional-set𝑖ℤ𝑖1\{i\in\mathbb{Z}:i\geq 1\}, and are truncatedat i=5,000𝑖5000i=5{,}000 and re-normalized: geometric distribution with parameter g=1/k𝑔1𝑘g=1/k satisfying pi∝(1−g)iproportional-tosubscript𝑝𝑖superscript1𝑔𝑖p_{i}\propto(1-g)^{i},Zipf distribution with parameter 1/2121/2 satisfying pi∝i−1/2proportional-tosubscript𝑝𝑖superscript𝑖12p_{i}\propto i^{-1/2},and log-series distribution with parameter γ=2/k𝛾2𝑘\gamma=2/k satisfying pi∝(1−γ)i/iproportional-tosubscript𝑝𝑖superscript1𝛾𝑖𝑖p_{i}\propto(1-\gamma)^{i}/i.

As shown below, the proposed PML approximation algorithm has exceptional performance.

We derive a new distribution estimator under the (unsorted) ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance by combining the proposed PML computation algorithm with the denoising procedure in [77] and a missing mass estimator [60].

First we describe this distribution estimator, which takes a sample Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n} from some unknown distribution p𝑝p. An optional input is 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X}, the underlying alphabet.

Apply the PML computation algorithm described in Section 4.1 to Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n},and denote the returned vector,consisting of non-negative real numbers that sum to 111, by V𝑉V.

Employ the following variant of the denoising procedure in [77].Arbitrarily remove a total probability mass of log−2⁡nsuperscript2𝑛\log^{-2}n fromentries of the vector V𝑉V without making any entry negative. Thenfor each j≤log2⁡n𝑗superscript2𝑛j\leq\log^{2}n,augment the vector byn/(j​log4⁡n)𝑛𝑗superscript4𝑛n/(j\log^{4}n) entries of probability j/n𝑗𝑛j/n.For every multiplicity μ≥1𝜇1\mu\geq 1 appearing in the sample,assign to all symbols appearing μ𝜇\mu times the following probability value.If μ≥log2⁡n𝜇superscript2𝑛\mu\geq\log^{2}n, simply assign to each of these symbolsthe empirical estimate μ/n𝜇𝑛\mu/n;otherwise, temporally associate a weight of bin​(n,v,μ):=(nμ)​(1−v)n−μ​vμassignbin𝑛𝑣𝜇binomial𝑛𝜇superscript1𝑣𝑛𝜇superscript𝑣𝜇\mathrm{bin}(n,v,\mu):=\binom{n}{\mu}(1-v)^{n-\mu}v^{\mu} with each entry v𝑣v in V𝑉V,and assign to each of these symbols the current weighted median of V𝑉V.

If 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X} is available, we can estimate the total probability mass M​(Xn):=∑x∈𝒳𝟙x∉Xnassign𝑀superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑥𝒳subscript1𝑥superscript𝑋𝑛M(X^{n}):=\sum_{x\in\mathcal{X}}\mathbbm{1}_{x\not\in X^{n}} of the unseen symbols (a.k.a., the missing mass) by the following estimator:M^​(Xn):=φ1​(Xn)∑j(j​φj​(Xn)​𝟙j>φj+1+(j+1)​φj+1​(Xn)​𝟙j≤φj+1).assign^𝑀superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝜑1superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑗𝑗subscript𝜑𝑗superscript𝑋𝑛subscript1𝑗subscript𝜑𝑗1𝑗1subscript𝜑𝑗1superscript𝑋𝑛subscript1𝑗subscript𝜑𝑗1\hat{M}(X^{n}):=\frac{\varphi_{1}(X^{n})}{\sum_{j}(j\varphi_{j}(X^{n})\mathbbm{1}_{j>\varphi_{j+1}}+(j+1)\varphi_{j+1}(X^{n})\mathbbm{1}_{j\leq\varphi_{j+1}})}.We equally distribute this probability mass estimate among symbols that do not appear in the sample.

As shown below, the proposed distribution estimator achieves the state-of-the-art performance.

In Figures 2, the horizontal axis reflects the sample sizen𝑛n, ranging from 10,0001000010{,}000 to 100,000100000100{,}000, and the vertical axisreflects the (unsorted) ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance between the truedistribution and the estimates, averaged over 303030 independenttrials. We compare our estimator with three others:the improved Good-Turing estimator [60],the empirical estimator, serving as a baseline, andthe empirical estimator with a larger n​log⁡n𝑛𝑛n\log n sample size.Note that log⁡n𝑛\log n is roughly 111111.As shown in [60],the improved Good-Turing estimatoris provably instance-by-instance near-optimal andsubstantially outperforms other estimators such as the Laplace(add-111) estimator, the Braess-Sauer estimator [13], andthe Krichevsky-Trofimov estimator [51]. Hence we do notinclude those estimators in our comparisons.

As the following plots show, our proposed estimator outperformed theimproved Good-Turing estimator in all experiments.

In Figure 3, the sample size n𝑛n ranges from 2,00020002{,}000 to20,0002000020{,}000, and the vertical axis reflects the sorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1}distance between the true distribution and the estimates, averagedover 303030 independent trials. We compare our estimator with thatproposed by Valiant and Valiant [79] that utilizes linear programming, withthe empirical estimator, and with the empirical estimator with a largern​log⁡n𝑛𝑛n\log n sample size.

We do not include the estimator in [41] since there is noimplementation available, and as pointed out by the recent work of [81] (page 7), the approach in [41] “is quite unwieldy. It involves significant parameter tuning and special treatment for the edge cases.” and “Some techniques …are quite crude and likely lose large constant factors both in theory and in practice.”

As shown in Figure 3, with the exception of uniformdistribution, where the estimator in Valiant and Valiant [79] (VV-LP) is thebest and PML is the closest second, the PML estimator outperformsVV-LP for all other tested distributions. As the underlying distribution becomes more skewed, the improvement of PML over VV-LP grows. For the log-series distribution, the performance of VV-LP is even worse than the empirical estimator.

Additionally, the plots also demonstrate that PML has a more stable performance than VV-LP.

In Figure 4, the sample size n𝑛n ranges from 1,00010001{,}000 to1,000,00010000001{,}000{,}000, and the vertical axis reflects the absolutedifference between the true entropy values and the estimates, averagedover 303030 independent trials. We compare our estimator with twostate-of-the-art estimators,WY in [84],and JVHW in [47],as well as the empirical estimator,and the empirical estimator with a larger n​log⁡n𝑛𝑛n\log n sample size. Additional entropy estimators such as theMiller-Mallow estimator [16], the best upper bound (BUB)estimator [66], and the Valiant-Valiantestimator [79] were compared in [84, 47] andfound to perform similarly to or worse than the two estimators that wecompared with, therefore we do not include themhere. Also, considering [79], page 50 in [88]notes that “the performance of linear programmingestimator starts to deteriorate when the sample size is very large.”

Note that the alphabet size k𝑘k is a crucial input to WY, but is notrequired by either JVHW or our PML algorithm. In the experiments, weprovide WY with the true value of k=5,000𝑘5000k=5{,}000.

As shown in the plots, our estimator performs as well as these state-of-the-art estimators.

For a distribution p∈Δ𝒳𝑝subscriptΔ𝒳p\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}, recall that the α𝛼\alpha-power sum of p𝑝p is Pα​(p)=∑xp​(x)αsubscript𝑃𝛼𝑝subscript𝑥𝑝superscript𝑥𝛼P_{\alpha}(p)=\sum_{x}p(x)^{\alpha}, implying Hα​(p)=(1−α)−1​log⁡(Pα​(p))subscript𝐻𝛼𝑝superscript1𝛼1subscript𝑃𝛼𝑝H_{\alpha}(p)=(1-\alpha)^{-1}\log(P_{\alpha}(p)). To establish the sample-complexity upper bounds mentioned in Section 3.3 for non-integer α𝛼\alpha values, Acharya et al. [6] first estimate the Pα​(p)subscript𝑃𝛼𝑝P_{\alpha}(p) using the α𝛼\alpha-power-sum estimator proposed in [47], and then substitute the estimate into the previous equation.The authors of [47] have implemented this two-step Rényi entropy estimation algorithm. In the experiments, we take a sample of size n𝑛n, ranging from 10,0001000010{,}000 to 100,000100000100{,}000, and compare our estimator with this implementation, referred to as JVHW, the empirical estimator, and the empirical estimator with a larger n​log⁡n𝑛𝑛n\log n sample size. Note that log⁡n𝑛\log n ranges from 9.29.29.2 to 11.511.511.5.According to the results in [6], the sample complexities for estimating α𝛼\alpha-Rényi entropy are quite different for α<1𝛼1\alpha<1 and α>1𝛼1\alpha>1, hence we consider two cases: α=0.5𝛼0.5\alpha=0.5 and α=1.5𝛼1.5\alpha=1.5.

As shown in Figure 5 and 6, our estimator clearly outperformed the one proposed by [6, 47].

We further note that for small sample sizes and several distributions, theestimator in [6, 47] performs significantly worse than ours. Also, for large sample sizes, the estimators in [6, 47] degenerates to the simple empirical plug-in estimator. In comparison, our proposed estimator tracks the performance of the empirical estimator with a larger n​log⁡n𝑛𝑛n\log n sample size for nearly all the tested distributions.

The proof proceeds as follows.First, fixing n𝑛n, 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X}, and a symmetric additive property f𝑓f that is 111-Lipschitz on (Δ𝒳,R)subscriptΔ𝒳𝑅(\Delta_{\mathcal{X}},R), we consider a related linear program defined in [80], and lower bound the worst-case error of any estimators using the linear program’s objective value, say v𝑣v.Second, following the construction in [80], we find an explicit estimator f^⋆superscript^𝑓⋆\hat{f}^{\star} that is linear, i.e., can be expressed as a linear combination of φisubscript𝜑𝑖\varphi_{i}’s, and show optimality by upper bounding its worst-case error in terms of v𝑣v.Third, we study the concentration of a general linear estimator, and through the McDiarmid’s inequality [56], relate the tail probability of its estimate to the estimator’s sensitivity to the input changes.Fourth, we bound the sensitivity of f^⋆superscript^𝑓⋆\hat{f}^{\star} by the maximum difference between its consecutive coefficients, and further bound this difference by a function of n𝑛n, showing that the estimate induced by f^⋆superscript^𝑓⋆\hat{f}^{\star} highly concentrates around its expectation.Finally, we invoke the result in [5] that the PML-plug-inestimator is competitive to all profile-based estimators whoseestimates are highly concentrated, concluding that PML shares theoptimality of f^⋆superscript^𝑓⋆\hat{f}^{\star}, thereby establishing Theorem 1.

Let f𝑓f be a symmetric additive property that is 111-Lipschitz on (Δ𝒳,R)subscriptΔ𝒳𝑅(\Delta_{\mathcal{X}},R). Without loss of generality, we assume that f​(p)=0𝑓𝑝0f(p)=0 if p​(x)=1𝑝𝑥1p(x)=1 for some x∈𝒳𝑥𝒳x\in\mathcal{X}.

First, fixing n𝑛n, 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X}, and f𝑓f, we lower bound the worst-case error of any estimators.

Let u∈(0,1/2)𝑢012u\in(0,1/2) be a small absolute constant. If there is an estimator f^^𝑓\hat{f} that, when given a length-n𝑛n sample from any distribution p∈Δ𝒳𝑝subscriptΔ𝒳p\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}, will estimate f​(p)𝑓𝑝f(p) up to an error of ε𝜀\varepsilon with probability at least 1/2+u12𝑢1/2+u.Then for any two distributions p1,p2∈Δ𝒳subscript𝑝1subscript𝑝2subscriptΔ𝒳p_{1},p_{2}\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}} satisfying |f​(p1)−f​(p2)|>ε𝑓subscript𝑝1𝑓subscript𝑝2𝜀|f(p_{1})-f(p_{2})|>\varepsilon, we can use f^^𝑓\hat{f} to distinguish Xn∼p1similar-tosuperscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑝1X^{n}\sim p_{1} from Xn∼p2similar-tosuperscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑝2X^{n}\sim p_{2}, and will be correct with probability at least 1/2+u12𝑢1/2+u.

On the other hand, for any parameter c1∈(1/100,1/25]subscript𝑐11100125c_{1}\in(1/100,1/25] and c2=1/2+6​c1subscript𝑐2126subscript𝑐1c_{2}=1/2+6c_{1}, consider the corresponding linear program defined in Linear Program 6.7 in [80], and denote by v𝑣v the objective value of any of its solutions. Then, Proposition 6.8 in [80] implies that we can find two distributions p1,p2∈Δ𝒳subscript𝑝1subscript𝑝2subscriptΔ𝒳p_{1},p_{2}\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}} such that |f​(p1)−f​(p2)|>v⋅(1−o​(1))−O​(n−c1​log⁡n)𝑓subscript𝑝1𝑓subscript𝑝2⋅𝑣1𝑜1𝑂superscript𝑛subscript𝑐1𝑛|f(p_{1})-f(p_{2})|>v\cdot(1-o(1))-O(n^{-c_{1}}\log n), and no algorithm can use Poi​(n)Poi𝑛\mathrm{Poi}(n) sample points to distinguish these two distributions with probability at least 1/2+u12𝑢1/2+u.

The previous reasoning yields that v<(1+o​(1))​ε+O​(n−c1​log⁡n)𝑣1𝑜1𝜀𝑂superscript𝑛subscript𝑐1𝑛v<(1+o(1))\varepsilon+O(n^{-c_{1}}\log n). By construction, v𝑣v is a function of 𝒳,n,𝒳𝑛\mathcal{X},n, and f𝑓f, and essentially serves as a lower bound for ε𝜀\varepsilon.

Second, fixing n𝑛n, 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X}, and f𝑓f, we construct an explicit estimator based on the previously mentioned linear program, and show optimality by upper bounding its worst-case error in terms of v𝑣v, the linear program’s objective value.

A property estimator f^^𝑓\hat{f} is linear if there exist real coefficients {ℓi}i≥1subscriptsubscriptℓ𝑖𝑖1\{\ell_{i}\}_{i\geq 1} such that the identity f^​(xn)=∑i≥1ℓi⋅φi​(xn)^𝑓superscript𝑥𝑛subscript𝑖1⋅subscriptℓ𝑖subscript𝜑𝑖superscript𝑥𝑛\hat{f}(x^{n})=\sum_{i\geq 1}\ell_{i}\cdot\varphi_{i}(x^{n}) holds for all xnsuperscript𝑥𝑛x^{n}. The following lemma (Proposition 6.10 in [80]) bounds the worst-case error of a linear estimator when its coefficients satisfy certain conditions.

Third, we slightly diverge from the previous discussion and study the concentration of general linear estimators.

By these two lemmas, we have the following result for the concentration of linear estimators.

A property estimator f^^𝑓\hat{f} is profile-based if there exists a mapping g^^𝑔\hat{g} such that f^​(xn)=g^​(φ​(xn))^𝑓superscript𝑥𝑛^𝑔𝜑superscript𝑥𝑛\hat{f}(x^{n})=\hat{g}(\varphi(x^{n})) for all xn∈𝒳∗superscript𝑥𝑛superscript𝒳x^{n}\in\mathcal{X}^{*}. The following lemma [2, 5, 27] states that the PML estimator is competitive to other profile-based estimators.

The factor exp⁡(3​n)3𝑛\exp(3\sqrt{n}) directly comes from the well-known result of Hardy and Ramanujan [45] on integer partitions,since there is a bijective mapping from profiles of size n𝑛n to partitions of integer n𝑛n.

Finally, we combine the above results and establish Theorem 1.

Specifically, if we increase the value of c1subscript𝑐1c_{1} to achieve a better lower bound on ε𝜀\varepsilon, the value of α𝛼\alpha may need to be reduced accordingly, which enlarges the sample complexity gap between our estimators and the optimal one. The proof of the following theorem is essentially the same as that shown in the previous section. However, for completeness, we still include a full-length proof.

For an integer α>1𝛼1\alpha>1, the following theorem characterizes the performance of the PML-plug-in estimator. For any p∈Δ𝒳𝑝subscriptΔ𝒳p\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}, ε∈(0,1)𝜀01\varepsilon\in(0,1), and a sample Xn∼psimilar-tosuperscript𝑋𝑛𝑝X^{n}\sim p with profile φ𝜑\varphi,

Due to the lower bounds in [6], for all possible values of α𝛼\alpha, the sample complexity of the PML plug-in estimator has the optimal dependency in k𝑘k.The remaining section is devoted to proving the above theorem. Note that estimating the Rényi entropy Hα​(p)subscript𝐻𝛼𝑝H_{\alpha}(p) to an additive error is equivalent to estimating the power sum Pα​(p)subscript𝑃𝛼𝑝P_{\alpha}(p) to a corresponding multiplicative error. Given this fact, we consider the estimator P^αsubscript^𝑃𝛼\hat{P}_{\alpha} in [6] that maps each sequence xn∈𝒳∗superscript𝑥𝑛superscript𝒳x^{n}\in\mathcal{X}^{*} toP^α​(xn):=∑xμx​(xn)α¯nα¯,assignsubscript^𝑃𝛼superscript𝑥𝑛subscript𝑥subscript𝜇𝑥superscriptsuperscript𝑥𝑛¯𝛼superscript𝑛¯𝛼\hat{P}_{\alpha}(x^{n}):=\sum_{x}\frac{\mu_{x}(x^{n})^{\underline{\alpha}}}{n^{\underline{\alpha}}},where for any real number z𝑧z, the expression zα¯superscript𝑧¯𝛼z^{\underline{\alpha}} denotes the falling factorial of z𝑧z to the power α𝛼\alpha.For a sample Xn∼psimilar-tosuperscript𝑋𝑛𝑝X^{n}\sim p, we have 𝔼​[P^α​(Xn)]=Pα​(p)𝔼delimited-[]subscript^𝑃𝛼superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑃𝛼𝑝\mathbb{E}[\hat{P}_{\alpha}(X^{n})]=P_{\alpha}(p). The following lemma [59, 6] states thatP^α​(Xn)subscript^𝑃𝛼superscript𝑋𝑛\hat{P}_{\alpha}(X^{n}) often estimates Pα​(p)subscript𝑃𝛼𝑝P_{\alpha}(p) to a small multiplicative error when n𝑛n is large.

The following corollary is a consequence of the above lemma, the sufficiency of profiles, and the standard median trick.

For convenience, we first restate the theorem.

In this section, we relate the estimation of sorted distributions to that of distribution properties through a dual definition of the 111-Wasserstein distance.

The reason is a little bit subtle: The inequality on |f​(p)−f​(pφ​(Xn/α))|𝑓𝑝𝑓subscript𝑝𝜑superscript𝑋𝑛𝛼|f(p)-f(p_{\varphi(X^{n/\alpha})})| holds for any fixed function f𝑓f and p∈Δ𝒳𝑝subscriptΔ𝒳p\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}, while the function that achieves the corresponding supremum insupf∈ℒ1|f​(p)−f​(pφ​(Xn/α))|=ℓ1<​(p,pφ​(Xn/α))subscriptsupremum𝑓subscriptℒ1𝑓𝑝𝑓subscript𝑝𝜑superscript𝑋𝑛𝛼superscriptsubscriptℓ1<𝑝subscript𝑝𝜑superscript𝑋𝑛𝛼\sup_{f\in\mathcal{L}_{1}}\left|f(p)-f(p_{\varphi(X^{n/\alpha})})\right|=\ell_{1}^{\text{\tiny{<}}}\left(p,p_{\varphi(X^{n/\alpha})}\right)depends on both p𝑝p and Xn/αsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝛼X^{n/\alpha}, and hence is a random function. To address this discrepancy, we provide a more involved argument below.

Let f𝑓f be a function in ℒ1subscriptℒ1\mathcal{L}_{1}. Without loss of generality, we also assume that f​(0)=0𝑓00f(0)=0. Let η∈(0,1)𝜂01\eta\in(0,1) be a threshold parameter to be determined later. For a parameter s>3𝑠3s>3 to be chosen later. The idea appearing in the last section also applies to other tasks. One of the extensions is to compute a truncated/partial PML and use the corresponding plug-in estimator to approximate certain properties.

The following lemma states that the TPML plug-in estimator is competitive to other truncated-profile-based estimators.

Estimator H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s} is not a profile-based estimator as the sample partitioning creates asymmetry.Therefore Lemma 10 does not directly apply here. To close this gap, we present two different approaches:one is to modify the definition of TPML and redefine it as the probability-maximizing distribution for sequence partitions,the other is to modify the estimator so that it is profile-based without changing the estimator’s bias and sensitivity too much.Below we present the first approach.

For any sequence pair (xm,ym)superscript𝑥𝑚superscript𝑦𝑚(x^{m},y^{m}), define the prevalence μi,j​(xm,ym)subscript𝜇𝑖𝑗superscript𝑥𝑚superscript𝑦𝑚\mu_{i,j}(x^{m},y^{m}) of an integer pair (i,j)𝑖𝑗(i,j) as the number of symbols x𝑥x satisfying both μx​(xm)=isubscript𝜇𝑥superscript𝑥𝑚𝑖\mu_{x}(x^{m})=i and μx​(ym)=jsubscript𝜇𝑥superscript𝑦𝑚𝑗\mu_{x}(y^{m})=j.We re-define the t𝑡t-truncated profile of (xm,ym)superscript𝑥𝑚superscript𝑦𝑚(x^{m},y^{m}) as the t×t𝑡𝑡t\times t matrixφt​(xm,ym):=[μi,j​(xm,ym)]i,j∈[t].assignsuperscript𝜑𝑡superscript𝑥𝑚superscript𝑦𝑚subscriptdelimited-[]subscript𝜇𝑖𝑗superscript𝑥𝑚superscript𝑦𝑚𝑖𝑗delimited-[]𝑡\varphi^{t}(x^{m},y^{m}):=[\mu_{i,j}(x^{m},y^{m})]_{i,j\in[t]}.In the same way we define the TPML estimator and derive a result similar to Lemma 10.

This creates a new version of TPML but does not change the nature of the approach.Later in this section, we provide an alternative argument employing the original TPML.

Due to the two indicator functions in the definition of H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s},we can view H^s​(Xn)superscript^𝐻𝑠superscript𝑋𝑛\hat{H}^{s}(X^{n}) as an estimator over (c4​log⁡(n/2))subscript𝑐4𝑛2(c_{4}\log(n/2))-truncated profiles.Then for any τ≥0𝜏0\tau\geq 0, together with the n𝑛n-sensitivitybound 𝒪​(nλ/n)𝒪superscript𝑛𝜆𝑛\mathcal{O}(n^{\lambda}/n) for H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s}, Lemma 4 yields thatPr⁡(|H^s​(Xn)−𝔼​[H^s​(Xn)]|≥τ)≤2​exp⁡(−2​τ2⋅Ω​(n1−2​λ)).Prsuperscript^𝐻𝑠superscript𝑋𝑛𝔼delimited-[]superscript^𝐻𝑠superscript𝑋𝑛𝜏2⋅2superscript𝜏2Ωsuperscript𝑛12𝜆\Pr\left(\left|\hat{H}^{s}(X^{n})-\mathbb{E}[\hat{H}^{s}(X^{n})]\right|\geq\tau\right)\leq 2\exp(-2\tau^{2}\cdot\Omega(n^{1-2\lambda})).The triangle inequality combines this with the previous bias bound,Pr⁡(|H^s​(Xn)−Hs​(p)|≥τ+𝒪​(kn​log⁡n))≤2​exp⁡(−2​τ2⋅Ω​(n1−2​λ)).Prsuperscript^𝐻𝑠superscript𝑋𝑛superscript𝐻𝑠𝑝𝜏𝒪𝑘𝑛𝑛2⋅2superscript𝜏2Ωsuperscript𝑛12𝜆\Pr\left(|\hat{H}^{s}(X^{n})-H^{s}(p)|\geq\tau+\mathcal{O}\left(\frac{k}{n\log n}\right)\right)\leq 2\exp(-2\tau^{2}\cdot\Omega(n^{1-2\lambda})).Applying Lemma 11 to H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s} with t=c4​log⁡(n/2)𝑡subscript𝑐4𝑛2t=c_{4}\log(n/2) further implies thatPr⁡(|Hs​(p)−Hs​(pφt)|≥2​τ+𝒪​(kn​log⁡n))≤2​exp⁡(−2​τ2⋅Ω​(n1−2​λ))⋅(n/2+1)2​t2.Prsuperscript𝐻𝑠𝑝superscript𝐻𝑠subscript𝑝superscript𝜑𝑡2𝜏𝒪𝑘𝑛𝑛⋅2⋅2superscript𝜏2Ωsuperscript𝑛12𝜆superscript𝑛212superscript𝑡2\Pr\left(|H^{s}(p)-H^{s}(p_{\varphi^{t}})|\geq 2\tau+\mathcal{O}\left(\frac{k}{n\log n}\right)\right)\leq 2\exp(-2\tau^{2}\cdot\Omega(n^{1-2\lambda}))\cdot(n/2+1)^{2t^{2}}.The right-hand side vanishes as fast as 2​exp⁡(−log3⁡n)2superscript3𝑛2\exp(-\log^{3}n) for τ=Ω​((log⁡n)1.5/n1/2−λ)𝜏Ωsuperscript𝑛1.5superscript𝑛12𝜆\tau=\Omega((\log n)^{1.5}/n^{1/2-\lambda}).

Note that we hide the estimator’s dependence on n𝑛n. Since λ𝜆\lambda is an arbitrary absolute constant in (0,1/2)012(0,1/2), the range of ε𝜀\varepsilon where the estimator is sample-optimal is near-optimal (e.g., set λ=0.01𝜆0.01\lambda=0.01) and better than the ε=Ω​(n−0.2)𝜀Ωsuperscript𝑛0.2\varepsilon=\Omega(n^{-0.2}) range established in [5] for the PML plug-in estimator.

We can view the estimator in Theorem 7 as a joint plug-in estimator of two distribution estimates: pφtsubscript𝑝subscript𝜑𝑡p_{\varphi_{t}} and pμsubscript𝑝𝜇p_{\mu}. Effectively, we decompose the original property into smooth and non-smooth parts. As is the case with PML and APML, for β∈(0,1)𝛽01\beta\in(0,1), we can define the β𝛽\beta-approximate TPMLestimator as a mapping from each truncated profile φtsuperscript𝜑𝑡\varphi^{t} to a distributionpφtβsuperscriptsubscript𝑝superscript𝜑𝑡𝛽p_{\varphi^{t}}^{\beta} satisfyingpφtβ​(φt)≥β⋅pφt​(φt)subscriptsuperscript𝑝𝛽superscript𝜑𝑡superscript𝜑𝑡⋅𝛽subscript𝑝superscript𝜑𝑡superscript𝜑𝑡{p}^{\beta}_{\varphi^{t}}({\varphi^{t}})\geq\beta\cdot p_{\varphi^{t}}({\varphi^{t}}). Via the same reasoning, one can verify that Theorem 7 also holds for any exp⁡(−polylogn)polylog𝑛\exp(-\operatorname*{polylog}n)-approximate TPML, which we refer to as ATPML.

The above derivation utilizes a modified TPML. We sketch an alternative argument [73] using the original version by modifying the estimator H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s} instead of TPML.

For a sample Xn∼psimilar-tosuperscript𝑋𝑛𝑝X^{n}\sim p, consider all its permuted versions. Applying H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s} to each permutation of Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n} yields an estimate. We define H^Ssuperscript^𝐻𝑆\hat{H}^{S} as an estimator that maps Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n} to the average of all such estimates. Averaging explicitly removes the estimator’s dependency on the ordering of sample points and makes it profile-based. In fact, this new estimator is over (c1+c2)​log⁡(n/2)subscript𝑐1subscript𝑐2𝑛2(c_{1}+c_{2})\log(n/2)-truncated profiles due to the two indicator functions in the definition of H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s}.

By symmetry and the linearity of expectation, the bias of H^Ssuperscript^𝐻𝑆\hat{H}^{S} in estimating Hs​(p)superscript𝐻𝑠𝑝H^{s}(p) is exactly equal to that of H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s}. In addition, any bounds on the sensitivity of H^ssuperscript^𝐻𝑠\hat{H}^{s} also applies to H^Ssuperscript^𝐻𝑆\hat{H}^{S}. In particular, for any absolute constant λ∈(0,1/2)𝜆012\lambda\in(0,1/2), we can choose a sufficiently small c2subscript𝑐2c_{2} so that the n𝑛n-sensitivity of H^Ssuperscript^𝐻𝑆\hat{H}^{S} is at most 𝒪​(nλ/n)𝒪superscript𝑛𝜆𝑛\mathcal{O}(n^{\lambda}/n).Utilizing Lemma 4, Lemma 10, and the same rationale as the previous argument, we establish Theorem 7 for the original version of TPML.

We can apply TPML and ATPML to approximate other symmetric properties having smoothness attributes similar to those of Shannon entropy.

This section revisits distribution estimation. Write max⁡{a,b}𝑎𝑏\max\{a,b\} as a∨b𝑎𝑏a\lor b. For any τ∈[0,1]𝜏01\tau\in[0,1], the τ𝜏\tau-truncated relative earth-mover distance [77], between p𝑝p andq𝑞q isRτ​(p,q):=infγ∈Γp,q𝔼(X,Y)∼γ|log⁡p​(X)∨τq​(Y)∨τ|.assignsubscript𝑅𝜏𝑝𝑞subscriptinfimum𝛾subscriptΓ𝑝𝑞subscript𝔼similar-to𝑋𝑌𝛾𝑝𝑋𝜏𝑞𝑌𝜏R_{\tau}(p,q):=\inf_{\gamma\in\Gamma_{p,q}}\;\operatorname*{\mathbb{E}}_{(X,Y)\sim\gamma}\left|\log\frac{p(X)\lor\tau}{q(Y)\lor\tau}\right|.

Define αn:=n.03+n.01assignsubscript𝛼𝑛superscript𝑛.03superscript𝑛.01\alpha_{n}:=n^{.03}+n^{.01}, βn:=n.03+2​n.01assignsubscript𝛽𝑛superscript𝑛.032superscript𝑛.01\beta_{n}:=n^{.03}+2n^{.01}, and γn:=αn/nassignsubscript𝛾𝑛subscript𝛼𝑛𝑛\gamma_{n}:=\alpha_{n}/n.The distribution estimator p^TPMLsubscript^𝑝TPML\hat{p}_{\text{\scalebox{0.7}{TPML}}} shown in Figure 7 is a simple combination of the TPML and empirical estimators, and satisfies

The estimator’s guarantee stated in Theorem 10 is essentially the same as that presented in [77]. The algorithms are different as our estimator is based on TPML, while the estimator in [77] mainly relies on a linear program. Unlike the latter, our approach additionally has the following desired attribute. For numerous symmetric properties, the single TPML estimator yields estimators that are sample-optimal over nearly all ranges of accuracy parameters. On the other hand, even just for support coverage, the method in [77] is known to offer sample-optimal estimators only when the desired accuracy is a constant.

Theorem 10 provides an estimation guarantee stronger than those appear in [75, 79], since the latter results degrade as the alphabet size increases. It is also of interest to derive a result similar to Theorem 5, which shows that both PML and APML are sample-optimal for learning sorted distributions. For any τ∈[0,1]𝜏01\tau\in[0,1], define the τ𝜏\tau-truncated sorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance between two distributions p,q∈Δ𝒳𝑝𝑞subscriptΔ𝒳p,q\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}} asℓτ<​(p,q):=minp′∈Δ𝒳:{p′}={p}​∑x|p′​(x)∨τ−q​(x)∨τ|.assignsuperscriptsubscriptℓ𝜏<𝑝𝑞subscript:superscript𝑝′subscriptΔ𝒳superscript𝑝′𝑝subscript𝑥superscript𝑝′𝑥𝜏𝑞𝑥𝜏\ell_{\tau}^{\text{\tiny{<}}}(p,q):=\min_{p^{\prime}\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}:\{p^{\prime}\}=\{p\}}\sum_{x}\left|p^{\prime}(x)\lor\tau-q(x)\lor\tau\right|.By Fact 1 in [77], given distributions p,q∈Δ𝒳𝑝𝑞subscriptΔ𝒳p,q\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}},ℓτ<​(p,q)≤2​Rτ​(p,q),superscriptsubscriptℓ𝜏<𝑝𝑞2subscript𝑅𝜏𝑝𝑞\ell_{\tau}^{\text{\tiny{<}}}(p,q)\leq 2R_{\tau}(p,q),implying

The proof essentially follows the proof of Theorem 2 in [77].The original reasoning is not sufficient for our purpose as the error probability derivedis too large to invoke the competitiveness of TPML.To address this issue, we slightly modify the linear program used in the paper, carefully separate the analysisof the estimators for large and small probabilities, and provide a finer analysis with tighter probability bounds by reducing the use of the union bound.To proceed, we first define histograms and the relative earth-moving cost, and give an operational meaning to Rτsubscript𝑅𝜏R_{\tau}.

For a distribution p𝑝p, the histogram of a multiset 𝒜⊆{p}𝒜𝑝\mathcal{A}\subseteq\{p\} is a mapping, denoted by h𝒜:(0,1]→ℤ≥0:subscriptℎ𝒜→01subscriptℤabsent0h_{{}_{\mathcal{A}}}:\!(0,1]\rightarrow\mathbb{Z}_{\geq 0}, that maps each number y∈(0,1]𝑦01y\in(0,1] to the number of times it appears in 𝒜𝒜\mathcal{A}. Note that every y𝑦y corresponds to a probability mass of y⋅h𝒜​(y)⋅𝑦subscriptℎ𝒜𝑦y\cdot h_{{}_{\mathcal{A}}}(y). More generally, we also allow generalized histograms hℎh with non-integral values h​(y)∈ℝ≥0ℎ𝑦subscriptℝabsent0h(y)\in\mathbb{R}_{\geq 0}. For any y1,y2∈(0,1]subscript𝑦1subscript𝑦201y_{1},y_{2}\in(0,1], generalized histogram hℎh, and nonnegative m<y1⋅h​(y1)𝑚⋅subscript𝑦1ℎsubscript𝑦1m<y_{1}\cdot h(y_{1}), we can move a probability mass from location y1subscript𝑦1y_{1} to y2subscript𝑦2y_{2} by reassigning h​(y1)−m/y1ℎsubscript𝑦1𝑚subscript𝑦1h(y_{1})-m/y_{1} to y1subscript𝑦1y_{1}, and h​(y2)+m/y2ℎsubscript𝑦2𝑚subscript𝑦2h(y_{2})+m/y_{2} to y2subscript𝑦2y_{2}.Given τ∈[0,1]𝜏01\tau\in[0,1],we define the cost associated with this operation ascτ,m​(y1,y2):=m⋅|log⁡y1∨τy2∨τ|,assignsubscript𝑐𝜏𝑚subscript𝑦1subscript𝑦2⋅𝑚subscript𝑦1𝜏subscript𝑦2𝜏c_{\tau,m}(y_{1},y_{2}):=m\cdot\left|\log\frac{y_{1}\lor\tau}{y_{2}\lor\tau}\right|,and term it as τ𝜏\tau-truncated earth-moving cost. The cost of multiple operations is additive.Under such formulation, Rτ​(p,q)subscript𝑅𝜏𝑝𝑞R_{\tau}(p,q) is exactly the minimal total τ𝜏\tau-truncated earth-moving cost associated with any operation schemes of moving h{p}subscriptℎ𝑝h_{\{p\}} to yield h{q}subscriptℎ𝑞h_{\{q\}}. Let ε𝜀\varepsilon be an arbitrary accuracy parameter and 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X} be a finite set. Let pusubscript𝑝𝑢p_{u} denote the uniform distribution over 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X}. Given sample access to an unknown distribution p∈Δ𝒳𝑝subscriptΔ𝒳p\in\Delta_{\mathcal{X}}, the uniformity testing distinguishes between the null hypothesisH0:p=pu:subscript𝐻0𝑝subscript𝑝𝑢H_{0}:p=p_{u}and the alternative hypothesisH1:∥p−pu∥1≥ε.:subscript𝐻1subscriptdelimited-∥∥𝑝subscript𝑝𝑢1𝜀H_{1}:\left\lVert p-p_{u}\right\rVert_{1}\geq\varepsilon.After a sequence of research works [35, 12, 67, 3, 17, 78, 29, 4, 28, 30], it is shown that to achieve a k−Θ​(1)superscript𝑘Θ1k^{-\Theta(1)} bound on the error probability, this task requires a worst-case sample size of order k​log⁡k/ε2𝑘𝑘superscript𝜀2\sqrt{k\log k}/\varepsilon^{2}. The uniformity tester TPML​(Xn)subscript𝑇PMLsuperscript𝑋𝑛T_{\text{\tiny PML}}(X^{n}) in Figure 9 is purely based on PML, and takes as input parameters k𝑘k and ε𝜀\varepsilon, and a sample Xn∼psimilar-tosuperscript𝑋𝑛𝑝X^{n}\sim p.

We studied three fundamental problems in statistical learning: distribution estimation, property estimation, and property testing.We established the profile maximum likelihood (PML) as the first universally sample-optimal approach for several important learning tasks: distribution estimation under the sorted ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance, additive property and Rényi entropy estimation, and identity testing.We proposed the truncated PML (TPML) and showed that simply combining the TPML and empirical estimates yields estimators fordistributions and their properties enjoying even stronger guarantees.

Several future directions are promising. We believe that neither the factor of 444 in the sample size in Theorem 1, nor the lower bounds on ε𝜀\varepsilon in Theorem 1, 5, and 6 are necessary. In other words, the original PML approach is universally sample-optimal for these tasks in all ranges of parameters. It is also of interest to extend the PML’s optimality to estimating symmetric properties not covered by Theorem 1 to 4, such as generalized distance to uniformity [10, 42], the ℓ1subscriptℓ1\ell_{1} distance between the unknown distribution and the closest uniform distribution over an arbitrary subset of 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X}.Besides the competitiveness we established for the PML-type estimators under the min-max estimation framework,  Hao et al. [44] and Hao and Orlitsky [43] recently proposed and studied a different formulation of competitive property estimation that aims to emulate the instance-by-instance performance of the widely used empirical plug-in estimator, using a smaller sample size. It is also meaningful to investigate the performance of PML-based techniques through this new formulation.
